	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Coal</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Coal_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Coal (disambiguation)">Coal (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A combustible sedimentary rock composed primarily of carbon</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Coal</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #cddeff"><a href="/wiki/Sedimentary" class="mw-redirect" title="Sedimentary">Sedimentary rock</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_bituminous.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Coal bituminous.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Coal_bituminous.jpg/258px-Coal_bituminous.jpg" decoding="async" width="258" height="221" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/20/Coal_bituminous.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="308" /></a><div><a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">Bituminous coal</a></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #ccc">Composition</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Primary</th><td><a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Secondary</th><td><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mineral_Lignito_GDFL028.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Mineral_Lignito_GDFL028.jpg/220px-Mineral_Lignito_GDFL028.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Mineral_Lignito_GDFL028.jpg/330px-Mineral_Lignito_GDFL028.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Mineral_Lignito_GDFL028.jpg/440px-Mineral_Lignito_GDFL028.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1168" data-file-height="875" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mineral_Lignito_GDFL028.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">Lignite</a> (brown coal)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_anthracite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Coal_anthracite.jpg/220px-Coal_anthracite.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Coal_anthracite.jpg/330px-Coal_anthracite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/72/Coal_anthracite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="337" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_anthracite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">Anthracite</a> (hard coal)</div></div></div>
<p><b>Coal</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Combustible" class="mw-redirect" title="Combustible">combustible</a> black or brownish-black <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a>, formed as <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">rock strata</a> called <b>coal seams</b>. Coal is mostly <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> with variable amounts of other elements; chiefly <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
Coal is formed when dead <a href="/wiki/Plant_matter" title="Plant matter">plant matter</a> decays into <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> and is converted into coal by the heat and pressure of deep burial over millions of years.<sup id="cite_ref-EIACoalExplained_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIACoalExplained-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Vast deposits of coal originates in former <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a>—called <a href="/wiki/Coal_forest" title="Coal forest">coal forests</a>—that covered much of the Earth's tropical land areas during the late <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvanian_(geology)" title="Pennsylvanian (geology)">Pennsylvanian</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> times.<sup id="cite_ref-ClealThomas2005_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClealThomas2005-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> burned for heat, coal supplies about a quarter of the world's <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> and two-fifths of its <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IEAstats2_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEAstats2-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> making and other industrial processes burn coal.
</p><p>The extraction and use of coal causes many premature deaths and much illness.<sup id="cite_ref-Heal_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heal-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Coal industry <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_coal_industry" title="Environmental impact of the coal industry">damages the environment</a>, including by <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> as it is the largest <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anthropogenic" class="extiw" title="wikt:anthropogenic">anthropogenic</a> source of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, 14 <a href="/wiki/Gigatonnes" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigatonnes">Gt</a> in 2016,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> which is 40% of the total fossil fuel emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-phys2018_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phys2018-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> As part of the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Energy_transition" title="Energy transition">energy transition</a> many countries have stopped using or use less coal, and the <a href="/wiki/UN_Secretary_General" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Secretary General">UN Secretary General</a> has asked governments to stop building new coal plants by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest consumer and importer of coal is <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_China" title="Coal in China">China</a>. <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal mining in China">China mines</a> almost half the world's coal, followed by <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_India" title="Coal mining in India">India</a> with about a tenth. <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Australia" title="Coal in Australia">Australia</a> accounts for about a third of world coal exports followed by <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Indonesia#Coal" title="Energy in Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Russia" title="Coal in Russia">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IEAstats_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEAstats-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#Cultural_usage"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Cultural usage</span></a></li>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word originally took the form <i>col</i> in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> *<i>kula</i>(<i>n</i>), which in turn is hypothesized to come from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> root *<i>g</i>(<i>e</i>)<i>u-lo-</i> "live coal".<sup id="cite_ref-etym_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etym-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> cognates include the <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> <i>kole</i>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> <i>cole</i>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <i>kool</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>chol</i>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>Kohle</i> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>kol</i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> word <i>gual</i> is also a cognate via the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> root.<sup id="cite_ref-etym_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etym-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span></h2>
<p>Coal is composed of <a href="/wiki/Macerals" class="mw-redirect" title="Macerals">macerals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minerals" class="mw-redirect" title="Minerals">minerals</a> and water.<sup id="cite_ref-BGS_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGS-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fossils" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossils">Fossils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> may be found in coal.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/220px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/330px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/440px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example chemical structure of coal</div></div></div>
<p>At various times in the geologic past, the Earth had dense forests<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> in low-lying wetland areas. Due to natural processes such as flooding, these forests were buried underneath soil. As more and more soil deposited over them, they were compressed. The temperature also rose as they sank deeper and deeper. As the process continued the plant matter was protected from <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a>, usually by mud or acidic water. This trapped the carbon in immense <a href="/wiki/Peat_bog" class="mw-redirect" title="Peat bog">peat bogs</a> that were eventually covered and deeply buried by sediments. Under high pressure and high temperature, dead vegetation was slowly converted to coal. The conversion of dead vegetation into coal is called <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/coalification" class="extiw" title="wikt:coalification">coalification</a>. Coalification starts with dead <a href="/wiki/Plant_matter" title="Plant matter">plant matter</a> decaying into <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>. Then over millions of years the heat and pressure of deep burial causes the loss of water, methane and carbon dioxide and an increase in the proportion of carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-BGS_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGS-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Thus first <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a> (also called "brown coal"), then <a href="/wiki/Sub-bituminous_coal" title="Sub-bituminous coal">sub-bituminous coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">bituminous coal</a>, and lastly <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite</a> (also called "hard coal" or "black coal") may be formed.<sup id="cite_ref-EIACoalExplained_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIACoalExplained-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The wide, shallow seas of the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous Period</a> provided ideal conditions for coal formation, although coal is known from most geological periods. The exception is the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event#Coal_Gap" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">coal gap</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, where coal is rare. Coal is known from <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> strata, which predate land plants—this coal is presumed to have originated from residues of algae.<sup id="cite_ref-Tyler1957_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler1957-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mancuso1981_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mancuso1981-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sometimes coal seams (also known as coal beds) are interbedded with other sediments in a <a href="/wiki/Cyclothem" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyclothem">cyclothem</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/220px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/330px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/440px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="2016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coastal exposure of the Point Aconi Seam in <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_Rank_USGS.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/290px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png" decoding="async" width="290" height="363" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/435px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/580px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png 2x" data-file-width="2132" data-file-height="2665" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_Rank_USGS.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal ranking system used by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a></div></div></div>
<p>As geological processes apply <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> to dead <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic material</a> over time, under suitable conditions, its <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_grade" class="mw-redirect" title="Metamorphic grade">metamorphic grade</a> or rank increases successively into:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">Peat</a>, a precursor of coal</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">Lignite</a>, or brown coal, the lowest rank of coal, most harmful to health,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> used almost exclusively as fuel for electric power generation
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jet_(lignite)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jet (lignite)">Jet</a>, a compact form of lignite, sometimes polished; used as an ornamental stone since the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Palaeolithic" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper Palaeolithic">Upper Palaeolithic</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sub-bituminous_coal" title="Sub-bituminous coal">Sub-bituminous coal</a>, whose properties range between those of lignite and those of bituminous coal, is used primarily as fuel for steam-electric power generation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">Bituminous coal</a>, a dense sedimentary rock, usually black, but sometimes dark brown, often with well-defined bands of bright and dull material It is used primarily as fuel in steam-electric power generation and to make <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">Anthracite</a>, the highest rank of coal is a harder, glossy black coal used primarily for residential and commercial <a href="/wiki/Space_heating" class="mw-redirect" title="Space heating">space heating</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">Graphite</a> is difficult to ignite and not commonly used as fuel; it is most used in pencils, or powdered for <a href="/wiki/Lubrication" title="Lubrication">lubrication</a>.</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cannel_coal" title="Cannel coal">Cannel coal</a> (sometimes called "candle coal") is a variety of fine-grained, high-rank coal with significant hydrogen content, which consists primarily of <a href="/wiki/Liptinite" title="Liptinite">liptinite</a>.
</p><p>There are several international standards for coal.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The classification of coal is generally based on the content of <a href="/wiki/Coal_volatiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal volatiles">volatiles</a>. However the most important distinction is between thermal coal (also known as steam coal), which is burnt to generate electricity via steam; and <a href="/wiki/Metallurgical_coal" title="Metallurgical coal">metallurgical coal</a> (also known as coking coal), which is burnt at high temperature to make <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>.
</p>
<h4><span id="Hilt.27s_law"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hilt's_law">Hilt's law</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hilt%27s_law" title="Hilt&#39;s law">Hilt's law</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hilt%27s_law" title="Hilt&#39;s law">Hilt's law</a> is a geological observation that (within a small area) the deeper the coal is found, the higher its rank (or grade). It applies if the thermal gradient is entirely vertical; however, <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">metamorphism</a> may cause lateral changes of rank, irrespective of depth.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_coal_mining" title="History of coal mining">History of coal mining</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/27/Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif/170px-Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" decoding="async" width="170" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/27/Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="212" data-file-height="330" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese coal miners in an illustration of the <i>Tiangong Kaiwu</i> encyclopedia, published in 1637</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest recognized use is from the <a href="/wiki/Shenyang" title="Shenyang">Shenyang</a> area of China where by 4000 BC <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> inhabitants had begun carving ornaments from black lignite.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Coal from the <a href="/wiki/Fushun" title="Fushun">Fushun</a> mine in northeastern China was used to smelt <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> as early as 1000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>, the Italian who traveled to China in the 13th century, described coal as "black stones&#160;... which burn like logs", and said coal was so plentiful, people could take three hot baths a week.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In Europe, the earliest reference to the use of coal as fuel is from the geological treatise <i>On stones</i> (Lap. 16) by the Greek scientist <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> (c. 371–287 BC):<sup id="cite_ref-Mattusch_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mattusch-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Among the materials that are dug because they are useful, those known as <i>anthrakes</i> [coals] are made of earth, and, once set on fire, they burn like charcoal. They are found in Liguria&#160;... and in Elis as one approaches Olympia by the mountain road; and they are used by those who work in metals.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Theophrastus, On Stones (16) <i>translation</i></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">Outcrop</a> coal was used in Britain during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> (3000–2000 BC), where it formed part of <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funeral</a> <a href="/wiki/Pyre" title="Pyre">pyres</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannicacoal_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannicacoal-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>, with the exception of two modern fields, "the <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Roman_Britain" title="Mining in Roman Britain">Romans</a> were exploiting coals in all the major coalfields in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> by the end of the second century AD".<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_1997,_322–324_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_1997,_322–324-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Evidence of trade in coal, dated to about AD 200, has been found at the <a href="/wiki/Heronbridge_Roman_Site" title="Heronbridge Roman Site">Roman settlement at Heronbridge</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Chester" title="Chester">Chester</a>; and in the <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">Fenlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a>, where coal from the <a href="/wiki/English_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="English Midlands">Midlands</a> was transported via the <a href="/wiki/Car_Dyke" title="Car Dyke">Car Dyke</a> for use in drying grain.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Coal cinders have been found in the hearths of <a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">villas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_forts" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman forts">Roman forts</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>, dated to around AD 400. In the west of England, contemporary writers described the wonder of a permanent brazier of coal on the altar of <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aquae_Sulis" title="Aquae Sulis">Aquae Sulis</a> (modern day <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>), although in fact easily accessible surface coal from what became the <a href="/wiki/Somerset_coalfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Somerset coalfield">Somerset coalfield</a> was in common use in quite lowly dwellings locally.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Evidence of coal's use for iron-working in the city during the Roman period has been found.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Eschweiler" title="Eschweiler">Eschweiler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a>, deposits of <a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">bituminous coal</a> were used by the Romans for the smelting of <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_1997,_322–324_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_1997,_322–324-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face,_Britain,_1942_D8263.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/220px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/330px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/440px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face,_Britain,_1942_D8263.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal miner in Britain, 1942</div></div></div>
<p>No evidence exists of the product being of great importance in Britain before about AD 1000, the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TCC_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">Mineral</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> coal came to be referred to as "seacoal" in the 13th century; the wharf where the material arrived in London was known as Seacoal Lane, so identified in a charter of <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">King Henry III</a> granted in 1253.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Initially, the name was given because much coal was found on the shore, having fallen from the exposed coal seams on cliffs above or washed out of underwater coal outcrops,<sup id="cite_ref-TCC_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> but by the time of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, it was understood to derive from the way it was carried to London by sea.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 1257–1259, coal from <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a> was shipped to London for the <a href="/wiki/Metalsmith" title="Metalsmith">smiths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">lime</a>-burners building <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TCC_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Seacoal Lane and Newcastle Lane, where coal was unloaded at wharves along the <a href="/wiki/River_Fleet" title="River Fleet">River Fleet</a>, still exist.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These easily accessible sources had largely become exhausted (or could not meet the growing demand) by the 13th century, when underground extraction by <a href="/wiki/Shaft_mining" title="Shaft mining">shaft mining</a> or <a href="/wiki/Adit" title="Adit">adits</a> was developed.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannicacoal_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannicacoal-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The alternative name was "pitcoal", because it came from mines. The development of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> led to the large-scale use of coal, as the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a> took over from the <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheel</a>. In 1700, five-sixths of the world's coal was mined in Britain. Britain would have run out of suitable sites for watermills by the 1830s if coal had not been available as a source of energy.<sup id="cite_ref-Wrigley_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wrigley-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In 1947 there were some 750,000 miners in Britain<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> but the last deep coal mine in the UK closed in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A grade between bituminous coal and anthracite was once known as "steam coal" as it was widely used as a fuel for <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotives</a>. In this specialized use, it is sometimes known as "sea coal" in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Small "steam coal", also called <i>dry small steam nuts</i> (or DSSN), was used as a fuel for domestic <a href="/wiki/Water_heating" title="Water heating">water heating</a>.
</p><p>Sea coal continues to accumulate on beaches around the world from both natural erosion of exposed coal seams and windswept spills from cargo ships. Many homes in such areas gather sea coal as a significant, and sometimes primary, source of home heating fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Scavenging sea coal for heating is still commonplace on both the Pacific and Atlantic coasts of the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emission_intensity">Emission intensity</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">Emission intensity</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">greenhouse gas emitted over the life of a generator</a> per unit of electricity generated. Of the currently widely used methods of generating electricity the emission intensity of coal and oil is high, as they emit around 1000g of CO2eq for each kWh generated; natural gas and pumped hydro are medium emission intensity at around 500g CO2eq per kWh; and all other methods are typically low emission intensity  of under 100g per kWh. The emission intensity of coal varies with type and generator technology and exceeds 1200g per kWh in some countries.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_density">Energy density</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Energy_value_of_coal" title="Energy value of coal">Energy value of coal</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of coal, that is its <a href="/wiki/Heating_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Heating value">heating value</a>, is roughly 24 <a href="/wiki/Megajoule" class="mw-redirect" title="Megajoule">megajoules</a> per kilogram<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> (approximately 6.7 <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hours" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt-hours">kilowatt-hours</a> per kg). For a coal power plant with a 40% efficiency, it takes an estimated 325&#160;kg (717&#160;lb) of coal to power a 100&#160;W lightbulb for one year.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>27.6% of world energy was supplied by coal in 2017 and Asia used almost three quarters of it.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coking_coal_and_use_of_coke_to_smelt_iron">Coking coal and use of coke to smelt iron</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">Coke (fuel)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/220px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/330px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/440px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3039" data-file-height="2136" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coke oven at a <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_fuel" title="Smokeless fuel">smokeless fuel</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, United Kingdom</div></div></div>
<p>Coke is a solid carbonaceous residue derived from <a href="/wiki/Coking_coal" class="mw-redirect" title="Coking coal">coking coal</a> (a low-ash, low-sulfur bituminous coal, also known as <i>metallurgical coal</i>), which is used in manufacturing <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> and other iron products.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Coal_Steel_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal_Steel-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Coke is made from coking coal by baking in an oven without oxygen at temperatures as high as 1,000&#160;°C, driving off the volatile constituents and fusing together the fixed carbon and residual ash. Metallurgical coke is used as a <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> and as a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> in <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>  The carbon monoxide produced by its combustion reduces <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a> (an <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a>) to <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.
</p><p>Waste carbon dioxide is also produced <span class="nowrap">(<span class="mwe-math-element" id="2Fe2O3 + 3C"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {2Fe2O3 + 3C -&gt; 4Fe + 3CO2}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mn>2</mn>

          <msubsup>
            <mtext>Fe</mtext>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mn>2</mn>
            </mrow>

      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {2Fe2O3 + 3C -&gt; 4Fe + 3CO2}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f48752473973ce8b21bd34db10ddeedea6072d35" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" id="2Fe2O3_+_3C" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:32.21ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {2Fe2O3 + 3C -&gt; 4Fe + 3CO2}}}"/></span>)</span> together with <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>, which is too rich in dissolved carbon so must be treated further to make steel.
</p><p>Coking coal should be low in ash, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, so that these do not migrate to the metal.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Coal_Steel_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal_Steel-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
The coke must be <a href="/wiki/Coke_strength_after_reaction" title="Coke strength after reaction">strong enough</a> to resist the weight of overburden in the blast furnace, which is why coking coal is so important in making steel using the conventional route. Coke from coal is grey, hard, and porous and has a heating value of 29.6 MJ/kg. Some cokemaking processes produce byproducts, including <a href="/wiki/Coal_tar" title="Coal tar">coal tar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, light oils, and <a href="/wiki/Coal_gas" title="Coal gas">coal gas</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Petroleum_coke" title="Petroleum coke">Petroleum coke</a> (petcoke) is the solid residue obtained in <a href="/wiki/Oil_refining" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil refining">oil refining</a>, which resembles coke but contains too many impurities to be useful in metallurgical applications.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_foundry_components">Use in foundry components <span id="Industrial_processes"></span><span id="Sea_coal"></span></span></h4>
<p>Finely ground bituminous coal, known in this application as sea coal, is a constituent of <a href="/wiki/Foundry_sand" class="mw-redirect" title="Foundry sand">foundry sand</a>. While the molten metal is in the <a href="/wiki/Molding_(process)" title="Molding (process)">mould</a>, the coal burns slowly, releasing <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing gases</a> at pressure, and so preventing the metal from penetrating the pores of the sand. It is also contained in 'mould wash', a paste or liquid with the same function applied to the mould before casting.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Sea coal can be mixed with the clay lining (the "bod") used for the bottom of a <a href="/wiki/Cupola_furnace" title="Cupola furnace">cupola furnace</a>. When heated, the coal decomposes and the bod becomes slightly friable, easing the process of breaking open holes for tapping the molten metal.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_coke">Alternatives to coke</span></h4>
<p>Scrap steel can be recycled in an <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnace</a>; and an alternative to making iron by smelting is <a href="/wiki/Direct_reduced_iron" title="Direct reduced iron">direct reduced iron</a>, where any carbonaceous fuel can be used to make sponge or pelletised iron. To lessen carbon dioxide emissions <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> can be used as the reducing agent<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> or waste as the source of carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gasification">Gasification</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">Coal gasification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Underground_coal_gasification" title="Underground coal gasification">Underground coal gasification</a></div>
<p>Coal gasification, as part of an <a href="/wiki/Integrated_gasification_combined_cycle" title="Integrated gasification combined cycle">integrated gasification combined cycle</a> (IGCC) coal-fired power station, is used to produce <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>, a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO) and the hydrogen (H<sub>2</sub>) gas to fire gas turbines to produce electricity. Syngas can also be converted into transportation fuels, such as <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a>, through the <a href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">Fischer-Tropsch process</a>; alternatively, syngas can be converted into <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, which can be blended into fuel directly or converted to gasoline via the methanol to gasoline process.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Gasification combined with Fischer-Tropsch technology is used by the <a href="/wiki/Sasol" title="Sasol">Sasol</a> chemical company of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> to make motor vehicle fuels from coal and natural gas.
</p><p>During gasification, the coal is mixed with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a> while also being heated and pressurized. During the reaction, oxygen and water molecules <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidize</a> the coal into carbon monoxide (CO), while also releasing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas (H<sub>2</sub>). This used to be done in underground coal mines, and also to make <a href="/wiki/Town_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Town gas">town gas</a> which was piped to customers to burn for illumination, heating, and cooking.
</p>
<dl><dd>3C (<i>as Coal</i>) + O<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O → H<sub>2</sub> + 3CO</dd></dl>
<p>If the refiner wants to produce gasoline, the syngas is routed into a Fischer-Tropsch reaction. This is known as indirect coal liquefaction. If hydrogen is the desired end-product, however, the syngas is fed into the <a href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a>, where more hydrogen is liberated:
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + H<sub>2</sub>O → CO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquefaction">Liquefaction</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal_liquefaction" title="Coal liquefaction">Coal liquefaction</a></div>
<p>Coal can be converted directly into <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuels</a> equivalent to gasoline or diesel by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbonization" title="Carbonization">carbonization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Coal liquefaction emits more carbon dioxide than liquid fuel production from <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>. Mixing in biomass and using CCS would emit slightly less than the oil process but at a high cost.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> State owned <a href="/wiki/China_Energy_Investment" title="China Energy Investment">China Energy Investment</a> runs a coal liquefaction plant and plans to build 2 more.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Coal liquefaction may also refer to the cargo hazard when shipping coal.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_of_chemicals">Production of chemicals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/330px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/495px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/660px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="478" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Production of chemicals from coal</div></div></div>
<p>Chemicals have been produced from coal since the 1950s. Coal can be used as a feedstock in the production of a wide range of chemical fertilizers and other chemical products. The main route to these products is <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>. Primary chemicals that are produced directly from the syngas include <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, which are the chemical building blocks from which a whole spectrum of derivative chemicals are manufactured, including <a href="/wiki/Olefins" class="mw-redirect" title="Olefins">olefins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> and others. The versatility of <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a> as a precursor to primary chemicals and high-value derivative products provides the option of using relatively inexpensive<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> coal to produce a wide range of valuable commodities.
</p><p>Because the slate of chemical products that can be made via coal gasification can in general also use feedstocks derived from <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, the chemical industry tends to use whatever feedstocks are most cost-effective. Therefore, interest in using coal tends to increase for higher oil and natural gas prices and during periods of high global economic growth that may strain oil and gas production. Also, production of chemicals from coal is of much higher interest in countries like South Africa, China and India<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> where there are abundant coal resources. The abundance of coal combined with lack of natural gas resources in China is a strong inducement for the coal to chemicals industry there. Similarly, Sasol has built and operated coal-to-chemicals facilities in South Africa.
</p><p>Coal to chemical processes require substantial quantities of water. Much coal to chemical production is in China<sup id="cite_ref-OILDRUM9371_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OILDRUM9371-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ICIS022712_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICIS022712-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> where coal dependent provinces such as <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> are struggling to control its pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_as_fuel_to_generate_electricity">Coal as fuel to generate electricity</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precombustion_treatment">Precombustion treatment</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Refined_coal" title="Refined coal">Refined coal</a></div>
<p>Refined coal is the product of a coal-upgrading technology that removes moisture and certain pollutants from lower-rank coals such as sub-bituminous and lignite (brown) coals. It is one form of several precombustion treatments and processes for coal that alter coal's characteristics before it is burned. Thermal efficiency improvements are achievable by improved pre-drying (especially relevant with high-moisture fuel such as lignite or biomass).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The goals of precombustion coal technologies are to increase efficiency and reduce emissions when the coal is burned. Precombustion technology can sometimes be used as a supplement to postcombustion technologies to control emissions from coal-fueled boilers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_plant_combustion">Power plant combustion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal-fired_power_station" title="Coal-fired power station">Coal-fired power station</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castle_Gate_Power_Plant,_Utah_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/220px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/330px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/440px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2293" data-file-height="1439" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Castle_Gate_Power_Plant,_Utah_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Castle Gate Power Plant">Castle Gate Power Plant</a> near <a href="/wiki/Helper,_Utah" title="Helper, Utah">Helper, Utah</a>, US</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg/220px-Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg/330px-Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg/440px-Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="897" data-file-height="634" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal rail cars</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_(winter_2017).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg/220px-Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg/330px-Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg/440px-Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4296" data-file-height="2352" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_(winter_2017).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bulldozer" title="Bulldozer">Bulldozer</a> pushing coal in <a href="/wiki/Ljubljana_Power_Station" title="Ljubljana Power Station">Ljubljana Power Station</a></div></div></div>
<p>Coal burnt as a <a href="/wiki/Solid_fuel" title="Solid fuel">solid fuel</a> in <a href="/wiki/List_of_coal_power_stations" title="List of coal power stations">coal power stations</a> to <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">generate electricity</a> is called thermal coal. Coal is also used to produce very high temperatures through combustion. Efforts around the world to reduce the use of coal have led some regions to switch to natural gas and electricity from lower carbon sources.
</p><p>When coal is used for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, it is usually pulverized and then burned in a <a href="/wiki/Furnace" title="Furnace">furnace</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Boiler" title="Boiler">boiler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Coal_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The furnace heat converts boiler water to <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a>, which is then used to spin <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbines</a> which turn <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical generator">generators</a> and create electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_efficiency" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermodynamic efficiency">thermodynamic efficiency</a> of this process varies between about 25% and 50% depending on the pre-combustion treatment, turbine technology (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_steam_generator" title="Supercritical steam generator">supercritical steam generator</a>) and the age of the plant.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A few <a href="/wiki/Integrated_gasification_combined_cycle" title="Integrated gasification combined cycle">integrated gasification combined cycle</a> (IGCC) power plants have been built, which burn coal more efficiently. Instead of pulverizing the coal and burning it directly as fuel in the steam-generating boiler, the <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal is gasified</a> to create <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>, which is burned in a <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbine</a> to produce electricity (just like natural gas is burned in a turbine). Hot exhaust gases from the turbine are used to raise steam in a <a href="/wiki/Heat_recovery_steam_generator" title="Heat recovery steam generator">heat recovery steam generator</a> which powers a supplemental <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>. The overall plant efficiency when used to provide <a href="/wiki/Combined_heat_and_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Combined heat and power">combined heat and power</a> can reach as much as 94%.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> IGCC power plants emit less local pollution than conventional pulverized coal-fueled plants; however the technology for <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> after gasification and before burning has so far proved to be too expensive to use with coal.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Other ways to use coal are as <a href="/wiki/Coal-water_slurry_fuel" title="Coal-water slurry fuel">coal-water slurry fuel</a> (CWS), which was developed in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, or in <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamic_generator" title="Magnetohydrodynamic generator">an MHD topping cycle</a>. However these are not widely used due to lack of profit.
</p><p>In 2017 38% of the world's electricity came from coal, the same percentage as 30 years previously.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In 2018 global installed capacity was 2<a href="/wiki/Terawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Terawatt">TW</a> (of which 1TW is in China) which was 30% of total electricity generation capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornot-Gandolfe_2018_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornot-Gandolfe_2018-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The most dependent major country is South Africa, with over 80% of its electricity generated by coal.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The total known deposits recoverable by current technologies, including highly polluting, low-energy content types of coal (i.e., lignite, bituminous), is sufficient for many years. On the other hand, much may have to be left in the ground to avoid climate change,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> so <a href="/wiki/Peak_coal" title="Peak coal">maximum use</a> could be reached sometime in the 2020s.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_industry">Coal industry</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main pages: <a href="/wiki/Category:Coal_companies" title="Category:Coal companies">Category:Coal companies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">Coal mining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_by_country" title="Coal by country">Coal by country</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal industry in China">Coal industry in China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal industry in Pakistan">Coal industry in Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal industry in India">Coal industry in India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coal_companies_of_Australia" title="Coal companies of Australia">Coal companies of Australia</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_mining">Coal mining</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">Coal mining</a></div>
<p>About 8000 Mt of coal are produced annually, about 90% of which is hard coal and 10% lignite. As of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Coal&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> just over half is from underground mines.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> More accidents occur during underground mining than surface mining. Not all countries publish <a href="/wiki/Mining_accident" title="Mining accident">mining accident</a> statistics so worldwide figures are uncertain, but it is thought that most deaths occur in <a href="/wiki/List_of_coal_mining_accidents_in_China" title="List of coal mining accidents in China">coal mining accidents in China</a>: in 2017 there were 375 coal mining related deaths in China.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Most coal mined is thermal coal (also called steam coal as it is used to make steam to generate electricity) but metallurgical coal (also called "metcoal" or "coking coal" as it is used to make coke to make iron) accounts for 10% to 15% of global coal use.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Legality_of_coal_mining">Legality of coal mining</span></h4>
<p>A court in Australia has cited climate change in ruling against a new coal mine.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_as_a_traded_commodity">Coal as a traded commodity</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal mining in China">China mines</a> almost half the world's coal, followed by <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_India" title="Coal mining in India">India</a> with about a tenth.<sup id="cite_ref-BPReview2016_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BPReview2016-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Australia" title="Coal in Australia">Australia</a> accounts for about a third of world coal exports, followed by <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Indonesia#Coal" title="Energy in Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Russia" title="Coal in Russia">Russia</a>; while the largest importers are <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Japan" title="Energy in Japan">Japan</a> and India.
</p><p>The price of metallurgical coal is volatile<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and much higher than the price of thermal coal because metallurgical coal must be lower in sulfur and requires more cleaning.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Coal futures contracts provide coal producers and the <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_industry" title="Electric power industry">electric power industry</a> an important tool for <a href="/wiki/Hedge_(finance)" title="Hedge (finance)">hedging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">risk management</a>.
</p><p>In some countries new onshore <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind</a> or <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar</a> generation already <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_electricity_by_source" title="Cost of electricity by source">costs</a> less than coal power from existing plants.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> However, for China this is forecast for the early 2020s<sup id="cite_ref-CarbTrk2018_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CarbTrk2018-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and for south-east Asia not until the late 2020s.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In India building new plants is uneconomic and, despite being subsidized, existing plants are losing market share to renewables.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_trends">Market trends</span></h3>
<p>Of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_coal_production" title="List of countries by coal production">countries which produce coal</a> China mines by far the most, almost half the world's coal, followed by less than 10% by India. China is also by far the largest consumer. Therefore, market trends depend on <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy_of_China" title="Energy policy of China">Chinese energy policy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA2018_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA2018-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Although the effort to reduce pollution means that the global long term trend is to burn less coal, the short and medium term trends may differ, in part due to Chinese financing of new coal-fired power plants in other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornot-Gandolfe_2018_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornot-Gandolfe_2018-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
